~~ Noon ~~

“Lapis, look!”

Lapis and Blue were flying near the top of their army’s defensive hemisphere. With the clear skies, they could see the entire battlefield from their vantage point, and all around below them, they could see explosions of light and colour. Lapis shrugged. “Well, I think we put on a better show for the reader!”

Though, they hadn’t recently. When the human army began their assault on Complex Alpha, they had kept in mind the danger of the fairy of flight. Most fairies avoided a direct confrontation with Lapis and Blue. Furthermore, due to the promise she had made with Blue, she wasn’t allowed to kill the human within the fairy. She relied on islands and plenty of airspace to save the human, just as she did with Antarc (and could have done with Scirocco), and the battlefield around Complex Alpha could not sufficiently provide either. As a result, they hadn’t gotten any “kills” since summer began. 

Now, as Blue enjoyed the light show below them, something caught her eye — a single speck of grey amidst the unblemished skies. She might have mistaken it for a dust speck on her visor if it didn't also appear on her radar at the same time. And unlike all the other fairies that faced them, the speck was headed towards them. 

Now, they were close enough that they could glimpse the entity’s features. It had the distinctive fairy crest on its chest, giving off a shine Blue could only describe as colourless. But its face and body were completely featureless, and it floated through the air without any thruster trails. “What is that?” Blue asked with sparkles in her eyes.

“No clue.” Lapis frowned slightly. “I’ve never seen it on the battlefield. It must not be a fairy they’ve deployed before.”

Lapis raised her <Gatling Gun> at it. But before Lapis could say anything, her and Blue heard a voice within their visor, similar to the telepathy they used. “Are you Lapis, commander of <Shadow>?”

“The one and only.” Lapis grinned. Blue thought for a moment that she’d introduce herself the same way she did to her, but Lapis chose to forgo it. “And what is your name, brave soldier?”

Though she hadn’t said it loudly — definitely not loud enough for her voice to cross the chasm of air between them, the fairy still responded. “I’m Echo.” Saying that, the fairy kneeled in midair, seemingly unconcerned with them. In a casual tone, almost as if he were a salesperson, he asked: “Do you have time to talk?”

And so, as the war raged on around them, the two fairies began making small talk.

~

It had been twenty minutes since Lapis accepted Echo’s invitation to talk. So far, they hadn’t talked about anything of importance — it had been small talk, the majority of which was Echo talking about his brother. But now, as Echo shifted the position of their legs, Lapis heard his voice echo in her visor: “Lapis, it confuses me how you are so strong with that fairy. I don’t understand.”

Lapis laughed. “Aww, that’s cute. I’ll take the compliment!” But Echo seemed to be completely serious as he continued: “You were chosen as commander of <Shadow> because of your combat capability, correct? Given your incredible results in the war as a Nightjar pilot, the best in the archipelago.” 

“I’m impressed that the humans have that info.” Lapis confirmed lightheartedly, something that Blue hadn’t known. In hindsight, there probably had to be a reason why the elves and vampires would pick someone as, for lack of a better word, weird, as Lapis. Echo continued: “You clearly have the skills and experience to excel at piloting a fairy.”

“But what confuses me is that no matter how skilled you are, piloting a fairy is like riding a bicycle. Once you learn it, it’s easy, but before that, there is a steep learning curve. Even someone like me, who is considered a prodigy, took two weeks to simply maintain stable flight, let alone touch the abilities. It is simply not possible to visualize how to control a fairy in a short time.”

“We’ve reached the monologue-y exposition part of the script,” Lapis complained to Blue, who was, despite Lapis’ grumbling, raptly listening to Echo’s words.  

“And yet, in that time, you were able to go head to head with Circe, one of our strongest fighters, and a few days after, you defeated Inferno. That should not be possible.”

Blue frowned at Echo’s words, while Lapis shrugged. “They’re right, I think.” She told Blue, “But they’re forgetting one thing.”

“Which is?”

“Plot armour!”

As Blue let out a cry of indignation, having expected an actual explanation from Lapis, the elf changed the subject. Speaking to Echo, she noted: “And what about you? For all your praise, you seem pretty confident. Even though this seems like your first deployment.”

“I suppose I am not unconfident. Though I am a far less experienced pilot, in terms of fairy strength alone, I am on the same level as Nova of the Stars in one-on-one combat.”

Lapis frowned once more. If that were true, then they would have been deployed far earlier. Unless… “A specialist?” She asked Echo. 

A short pause, and then Echo nodded. “Correct. My attacks can only affect fairies. I don’t know why that is the case, but…”

As they said those words, the sky, which had been a vast piece of blue the instant before, disappeared beneath countless writhing beings, grasping for Lapis and Blue with their formless hands. Each shape had a voice, and each one said the same thing. An echo of the others. 

“...Eternity said phantoms only affect the soul.”

…

…

The sky was a vivid, unblemished blue. Not a trace of their enemy lingered in the air, as if they were a ghost. As Lapis and Blue spun upwards slowly, taking in their empty surroundings, Lapis spoke softly: “See you on the battlefield.”

 “They ran away.” Blue said sourly, and in response Lapis nodded. “Yeah. But they’ll be back.”

Now, they took a look from their vantage point. Their defense towards the west, east, and north were holding strong. But to the south, at a flight level a few thousand feet below them, the elves and vampires assigned there had begun falling back. Blue could catch glimpses of what seemed like tentacles flailing from a grotesque, mouth-like singularity, living scythes that rapidly honed on the vampires without mercy. It was the work of one of humanity’s trump cards — the fairy of poison. Today, they had assigned the fairy to the south, and it was proving an effective adjustment. 

Lapis studied the scene silently, before muttering: “Guess I’ll do that.” In the blink of an eye, she switched out of mech form, and Blue found herself freefalling through the air at thirty thousand feet, the wind rushing through her holographic wings. 

As Lapis also fell through the air, she took out her radio from her cloak. “Get ten Nightjars and stop by Oni Island. We’re initiating Code Name: Windbreak. Cool name, right?”

“No, it’s not!” 

Blue recalled Lapis telling her about that before. Lapis sighed in disapproval, clearly disagreeing with Blue’s assessment, and tucked her radio back into her pocket. With a dramatic flourish, she switched back to mech form and headed south. 

~

Akito flew northwards over the sparkling sea. He had just departed Complex Zeta, and he was heading back to the northern front of Complex Alpha. As he headed towards the front lines, Minji’s voice sounded clearly in his visor. “You’ve been assigned the lowest flight level, 5 degrees North.”

The human army had relegated the majority of their squadron commanders to the front lines, but Minji had stayed in her typical non-combat role. Part of it was because her fairy was an exceedingly weak fighter, but also, her experience made her far more valuable as a supporter and leader. Over the past week, she had connected her fairy to Chiaros. This not only allowed her to view the war firsthand, but also meant she could advise Akito, since he lacked the combat experience everyone else did. 

On the second point, Akito felt extremely apologetic that she was forced to devote her time to him. But even he knew it was a necessity — only now, over three months after Akito met Chiaros, was he starting to get used to piloting him. It seemed piloting a fairy was easy only after a steep learning curve.

As he issued a silent word of gratitude to Minji, he began to repeat her instructions back: “Flight level 1, 5 degrees —”

“Sorry, one second.” Minji cut in.

That’s unusual, Akito thought to himself. Silence settled within his visor until Minji spoke up again. Her normally emotionless voice was clearly a bit shaken, though she maintained the same professionalism. “Akito, I’ll need to disconnect. You’ll be on your own today,” 

“What came up?” Akito asked, his curiosity getting the better of him. Minji was silent for a few more moments, confirming the news, before replying: “<Shadow> is targeting the place I’m in. I’ll need to evacuate.”

“Aren’t you in Complex Zeta?” Akito asked in confusion. It shouldn’t have been possible for the enemy to get past their defensive lines and reach the Complex.. But Minji shook her head. “I’m in Base 33. They’ll reach here and destroy it in a few minutes, so —.”

“Destroy? What about our defenses?” The human army had kept some fairies on patrol for counterattacks such as this.

“They broke through. I don’t have time to explain how, but…” Minji trailed off, conversing with someone external to her connection with Akito, before hurriedly adding: “I need to go.”

“I’ll head over!” Akito began. But Minji replied forcefully: “Don’t. Maintain your current heading. The sooner you get to the front lines, the better.”

Akito didn’t even have time to say he understood before Minji disconnected, leaving him alone above the open ocean. 

~

As part of Code Name: Windbreak, a sizable number of Nightjars fanned out across the ocean. Their destinations were ten of humanity’s bases. Lapis had broken up the south side of their defensive hemisphere, shrunk its radius, and used the remaining forces to counterattack. It was only a strategy that worked when the human army didn’t expect it, and when they had overcommitted on offense. 

In each Nightjar, there was an elf piloting and a vampire as a passenger, or payload of sorts. The Nightjars would avoid fighting the fairy patrols. Instead, they would quickly transport the vampires to the human bases and drop them off, and from there the vampire would easily be able to destroy the base and escape. Today, there was another passenger in each Nightjar, serving as a similarly destructive payload: an Oni. It had been a surprise for them. Because Lapis still didn’t trust them, she hadn’t given them any prior warning.

The bases they had chosen were at random. Lapis had thought for a long time about how to avoid Base 33. But as she didn’t know their location, nor the system they used to correspond the base number with the location, she had been forced to hope that Base 33 wasn’t chosen. 

Unfortunately for Lapis, at that moment, a Nightjar was soaring across the water towards Base 33. An occurrence which was even more unfortunate for the two people rapidly evacuating the base.

Minji and Algernon Prischett ran outside, each carrying crates of manila folders in their hand. They contained the most important portions of Minji’s work. As Algernon placed his crate beside the large pile of folders they had amassed a safe distance outside Base 33, he cursed himself for being a step behind. Today he had visited Base 33 in the hopes of asking Minji to relocate her work, but by then he was a step too late.

“Is that everything?” Minji asked, out of breath. They had managed to save everything Algernon deemed necessary from Minji’s work. Even still, he turned to Minji and shook his head. “One more thing. Wait right here.”

Algernon sprinted back the way they had come from, snapping the sticks beneath his feet as he made his way through the forest floor. The cozy wooden cabin entered his field of view. Through the open window, he could already see his target: a collection of prisms and mirrors, catching the noon sunlight that shone down overhead. Minji had said they weren’t important as they evacuated, but Algernon knew otherwise. 

Minji and Tanner were two of the few people whose name he remembered. Algernon would be damned if he failed to save something precious to them. 

But before Algernon could reach Base 33, the sunlight was blotted out by an utter darkness. The sticks at his feet swirled into the sky, taken in by the powerful beating of massive wings, and as Algernon glared hatefully upwards, a blood-red eye stared back at him. The vampire flexed his massive wings, his grotesque talons, and the general of the human army curled his lips in a cruel smile.

“Fucking vermin.”

Algernon only had those two words to mutter as the vampire dove towards him. A torrent of wind, unleashed by the beating of the vampire’s wings, spiralled downwards towards the clearing, sweeping up everything in its path. 

From outside that shrieking maelstrom, an archer took an arrow from his quiver. 

…

…

This world is known to have five dimensions. Three of them are spatial.

The fourth flows continuously, never ceasing. And the guise it takes is that of the fairy flying in the skies above Base 33 at that moment.

He was clothed in a tunic the colour of amber. In his chest was a fairy crest of the same vivid hue, and though a noticeable chunk of the crest was missing, what remained glowed radiantly, unfaltering. Various timepieces adorned the fairy: a stone sundial levitating above his head like an angel’s halo, four pocket watches affixed to his belt, and a luminous ring, as large as the fairy himself, centered in front. On that ring, the roman numerals from one to twelve were engraved.

A bow, in the shape of a clock. 

Now, the fairy drew back his arrow, one ten times larger than that for a regular bow, and took aim. Bowstrings of light reached out from the ring and wrapped around the arrow’s nock. That light, the same light illuminating the bow and arrow, was so pure it seemed like it wasn’t made from the constraints of the three dimensions of space. 

Piloting the fairy, Akito spoke in a low growl. “Don’t you dare.” He released the bowstring. The arrow flew unwaveringly straight through the whirlwind, piercing the vampire in the side before penetrating through Base 33 into the ground as if there were nothing there. There was no trace left behind, no change in the wooden cabin nor the landscape where the arrow had impacted. 

But a gaping hole had opened in the vampire’s side, one wide enough that Algernon could see through it to the other side. Blood spilled from the wound, drenching the clearing in red. Although it wasn't close to fatal — already, the edges of the wound were regenerating rapidly — the vampire turned his attention to Akito and his fairy and climbed upwards.

Akito reached for another arrow. There was a defiant glint in his eyes, and though the vampire couldn’t have seen Akito’s gaze, at that moment, a shiver ran down his spine. 

“You have no right to destroy that place.”

…

…

The fourth dimension of this world is time. It is the attribute of Chiaros, the fairy archer.

After all, time flies like an arrow. 

~

---------------------------------------------------------

LAPISNOTES

CHIAROS OF TIME

Propulsion System: Normal
Speed: Normal
Unique Traits: The fourth dimension. Has an attack which cannot be blocked by any physical object.
Name: Sounds cool

Attack:
<Yori no Mirai>
Fires an arrow which is not affected by the spatial dimension (i.e. cannot be blocked); only deals damage to alive beings (includes fairies)
Short reload, medium unload, long range, damage depends on where the arrow hits

Defense:
<Sempiternity>
User stops time in a ten meter radius for four seconds. Anything within that radius maintains their position when <Senpiternity> was activated. 
Velocity, momentum, and similar physical properties are also conserved. 
Only the user is allowed to move. This includes using their thruster (in which case, the area of effect for <Senpiternity> moves with them), using <Yori no Mirai> and using <Chrononeia Retrograde>. Furthermore, the user can move other objects (though velocity etc. once again will still be conserved)
Very slow reload, very fast unload

Misc:
<Chrononeia Retrograde> (Utility)
Unactivated, it is an orb that the user can retrieve by touching one of the pocket watches on the belt. The user can then attach this orb on any physical object or on <Yori no Mirai>
Is activated by the user flipping the pocket watch corresponding to the orb, Then, any particle within a three meter radius is sent back three seconds in time. This includes particles that are bonded or attached to other particles, even if that other particle is not within the area of effect of <Chrononeia Retrograde>

---------------------------------------------------------
As the vampire rushed towards Akito, the air surrounding Chiaros’ bow sparkled. Then, in an explosion of light, Akito released his arrow at the vampire’s bat-like head. 

Unlike every other fairy attack, <Yori no Mirai> was not a physical phenomenon; it wasn’t of the three spatial dimensions. That meant it couldn’t be blocked by physical barriers, and it only affected living organisms. Even against the strengthened body of the vampire, which couldn’t be dented by normal swords and handguns, <Placeholder> passed straight through, drilling a hole to the other side of the vampire’s skull. 

Though, that wasn’t nearly enough to kill him. Before his head had even fully regenerated, the vampire unleashed a massive swipe that Akito narrowly sidestepped. He lost his bearing midair, and as the sky tumbled past his visor, a flash of bat wings darted into his peripherals. The vampire raised his right claw and brought it down, using his superstrength to slam Akito into the forest floor below. 

Agh, sorry, I really can’t get used to how fast fighting is. Registering his health drop by a tenth, Akito cursed and apologized to no one in particular. He plummeted through the canopy of trees. Amidst the kaleidoscope of light caused by the sunlight filtering through the summer cypresses, Akito caught a glimpse of the vampire diving down towards him like an eagle catching up to his prey. Akito willed his right hand to move, fumbling around near the belt on his torso. 

<Chrononeia Retrograde>! 

Akito rotated one of the pocket watches on his belt. There was a mechanical click as its second hand stopped, wavered, then reversed direction. Before Akito’s mind could register how he got there, he was back in the sky, in the exact position he was in three seconds ago. <Chrononeia Retrograde> was an orb that allowed him to turn back time three seconds for particles within a certain radius, and he had just done so to himself. Now, as the vampire’s eyes flashed in confusion, searching for Chiaros, who had just disappeared from his field of vision, Akito aimed his bow down at him.

Take this! 

Another flash of light. The arrow pierced the vampire’s exposed wing. He lost control of his flight and crashed into the forest, snapping the cypresses in two as if they were mere sticks. 

That should put the vampire out of commission for a few seconds

Akito didn’t know it, but all he had to do was hold off the enemy long enough for the human army patrols to arrive. The Nightjar carrying the vampire hadn’t engaged with the patrols, let alone defeated them. Consequently, the patrol group was headed to Base 33 to engage them. It would take them one minute to get there. 

Though that was impossible. Because at that moment, a moss green flash raced through the undergrowth. Despite the oni’s massive size, they were running even faster than most fairies could fly. Akito barely had time to register the oni’s existence before they leapt across the clearing, drawing back their fist in the same moment. 

Minji knew it was impossible. Even if Akito were simply taking on a Nightjar and vampire, for one minute without any extra burdens, he’d be hard-pressed to do so given his inexperience. But Akito had come back to protect Base 33. Not only was that a battlefield on the ground, where he’d have to contend with the oni, but he’d have to defend a large, immobile target. That was why she had told him to head to the frontlines immediately, in the hopes of capitalizing on <Shadow> expending a portion of their force on Code Name: Windbreak. Everyone, from Minji to Algernon to the patrol force, had given up on saving the insignificant cabin that was Base 33. 

But, Akito didn’t care. About the impossibility, nor anything else.

Don’t you dare. 

<Yori no Mirai>!

A shiver ran down the spine of the oni’s back. He looked up to see an arrow hurtle towards him, carried not by the wind but by the yearning flow of a fourth dimension. There had been no sound or sign of the arrow being fired, and so the oni didn’t have enough time to react. Even so, the arrow missed, shooting through the air a few meters away from the oni’s body. But then, Akito flipped one of the pocket watches on his belt.

<Chrononeia Retrograde>!

<Chrononeia Retrograde> was a physical orb, one which could be attached to anything. But it could also be attached to one entity that wasn’t physical. Akito’s arrows, <Yori no Mirai>. 

Now, the orb Akito attached to his arrow activated. All the molecules in a radius a few meters away from it reset to their position and velocity three seconds ago. 

This included the oni’s outstretched arm and the left side of his body. Those parts of the oni travelled back three seconds in time, separating from the rest of his body, and landed harmlessly on the clearing a few meters away from the Base. It was as if someone had bisected the oni perfectly, leaving no jagged edges or rough wounds behind. 

With one remaining leg, the oni stumbled and fell. They shared the same regeneration and strengthened bodily traits that the vampires had, and so, as Akito rushed between the oni and the base, the oni lunged forward on his regrowing stump of a leg and swung at Akito with his other arm. Akito sidestepped it easily. And then, the oni let out a ferocious roar, and Akito realized his mistake. The oni wasn’t aiming to hit him, but rather to hit the cabin behind him. 

Ughh, sorry! I messed up…

Tanner had advised him to use his last ability sparingly because of its incredibly long reload time. But right now, Akito was forced to use it. 

I have to use <Sempiternity>.

…

…

…Time stops.

The clearing falls silent. Akito can no longer hear the rustling of the leaves, the cracking of branches and leaves underneath their feet, nor the oni’s roar. It is because none of those things are happening anymore. 

Sempiternity, an everlasting, infinite present. With the exception of Akito and his weapons, the entire clearing is frozen in time for a few seconds, as if someone has taken a photo with a camera and replaced the world with it. 

The oni can only helplessly watch, unable to move, as Akito uses <Chrononeia Retrograde> to send the oni back three seconds. Then, for good measure, he shoots two arrows into the oni’s limbs, leaving gaping holes where they once were.

That should be one down…

Time continues its unceasing flow…

As Akito thought that, <Sempiternity> unravelled. Sounds flooded back into Akito’s ears, and he surveyed his surroundings. The base was mostly intact. Twenty meters away, the oni lay lifelessly a safe distance away, blood pooling out of his wounds. Though Akito wasn’t sure if the oni was actually dead, given his physical constitution, he was confident the injuries were severe enough that the oni wouldn’t be a factor in the rest of the fight. He scanned the trees for any enemies. 

And then, amidst the sounds of the leaves rustling, he heard another sound. A low, mechanical whir. 

A few hundred feet above Base 33, a pale blue light began glowing intensely. The railgun of a Nightjar powered up. It was pointed straight at the cabin in the woods. 

“...”

Akito considered his options, working amidst the swirl of thoughts that suddenly raced through his mind. <Sempiternity> had just been used. <Chrononeia Retrograde> wouldn’t work on railguns, given that they were energy beams and not projectiles. <Yori no Mirai> would simply pass through the physical realm as if it didn’t exist. 

He was out of options. Out of time too, because just then, the Nightjar fired their railgun.

…

…

Algernon leapt over the fallen branches of the cypress trees in the north side of the forest. In his pockets were the collection of prisms and mirrors —  he had taken advantage of the fairy’s efforts to rush into the base and grab the collection. But instead of heading into the forest back towards Minji, he looked left, then right, searching for something. A flash of amber caught his eye. He quickly ran to the fairy of time.

The boy whose name he did not know had already gotten up, though he was clearly dazed. He was looking past Algernon. No doubt, he was looking at Base 33. Though the wooden cabin should have been decimated a few seconds prior, it was still standing, only slightly scratched from the debris. The reason for that was simple. 

Chiaros, or the boy piloting him, had taken the full extent of the railgun blast for himself, protecting the cabin. Algernon supposed it wasn’t that harmful to the boy, given that he felt no pain from it, but even still, no sane person would have done it. Especially given how futile it was. A low whir had permeated the air once more, as the Nightjar readied their second railgun charge. 

Algernon knew the boy didn’t have enough health to tank another hit. But even still, as he dragged the boy away from the cabin, the boy began resisting. Algernon stared at the boy for a moment. And then he hissed: “Do you think you get a gold medal of honour or something if you save that cabin? You get nothing, no accolades, no money, nothing if that thing is still standing. So don’t fucking bother. Because you aren’t going to save it.”

“Let go of my hand.”

A low voice, nearly a growl. Algernon narrowed his eyes, but before he could say anything, the boy continued. “Minji and Tanner’s home will not be destroyed. It is not your role in the story to decide otherwise, nor is it theirs. So let go.”

If the alternative was something worse, Akito was willing to keep his mouth shut. But here, the two choices were between protecting Base 33 and not protecting it. That made things easy. 

“...”

So many words piled up in Algernon’s throat at that moment. How the boy was foolish, how he would fail, how inane he was that he could even dare to say that it was his choice. But none of them escaped his mouth. 

Because at that moment, through the fairy’s visor, Algernon caught a glimpse of the boy’s eyes. Within those eyes was a belief. A weak, flickering belief, but one still there nonetheless. A belief so outlandish Algernon should have scoffed.

Those eyes proclaimed that this boy would lead his allies to a better future. 

“...”

Algernon shouldn’t have believed in that proclamation.

But for some reason, he let go of the boy’s arm. He watched as the boy drew his bow, took an arrow from his quiver. Then, the boy withdrew an orb of light from his pocket watch before affixing it onto his arrow. He took aim, directly at the Nightjar. Algernon didn’t know what the boy was planning, and truth to be told, he wouldn’t be surprised if the boy didn’t have a plan. 

Even still, Algernon believed it would work. 

A beam of pale blue light shot out towards the cabin. Akito readied his bow. 

Not yet…Not yet…

But just before he released his arrow, a large silver panel formed above the cabin. A mirror, reflecting the railgun beam and redirecting it harmlessly upwards. 

Akito and Algernon whirled around. Minji, transformed into the fairy of light, was behind them, arm outstretched to cast the mirror. As the railgun beam vanished into the sky, she turned to meet their surprised gaze. The fairy’s visor did nothing to dampen the glare she gave Akito. “I overestimated your intelligence,” she snapped icily, clearly unhappy to see Akito here. 

“S-sorry! I’m really sorry!” Akito stammered, instantly apologizing. But then he grinned. “Though, I’m glad to hear it.” 

The Nightjar suddenly veered off, powering up their thrusters. In the distance, Akito could see the fairy patrol finally arriving, and they began to give chase to the Nightjar pilot. As the sky above Base 33 finally cleared, Akito let out a happy breath, one which he didn’t even know he was holding in. “I guess —”

But just then, a shout came from beside them. It was Algernon. He had withdrawn his handgun from his coat pocket and was aiming it at Akito. 

No…behind me?

At that moment, the vampire tackled Akito from behind. He entangled him in his claws and carried the fairy with him, barrelling a destructive path through the cypress trees. 

~

The vampire flew under the tree cover, having regenerated his wings and the wound in his side. Ignoring the cypresses obstructing his path as if they were blades of grass to be pushed aside and trampled underfoot, he stretched out his claws like a bird of prey catching fish, dragging Akito and Chiaros over the gnarly tree roots and undergrowth. Until finally, he slammed Akito against a large cypress and took hold of his limbs. As if Akito were a bag of chips, he began pulling in opposite directions. 

And against vampiric superstrength, a fairy might as well have been a bag of chips. Akito watched as his health dropped from thirty percent to twenty in a matter of seconds. Frantically, he checked his visor. <Senpiternity> was still seconds away from reloading, seconds which he didn’t have. 

My bow. I need my bow and arrow.

He started to reach for his quiver, and quickly realized he couldn’t move an inch. The vampire had pinned his arms down to the forest floor, and it felt like the weight of the universe itself had been piled onto them. <Chrononeia Retrograde> needed his hands too, to flip the pocket watch. 

Splayed out on all fours, Akito found his abilities utterly useless. The health bar on his visor changed from orange to red. 

And yet, Akito found his mind surprisingly clear. Perhaps it was because Chiaros was absorbing all the pain he should have felt, but he calmly went through his course of actions. Nothing came to mind immediately, but a stray thought caught his attention — something Minji had shared a few weeks ago. 

“Fairies are containers to preserve the souls of ancient humans.”

That was a convoluted way of saying that fairies were the souls of ancient humans stored in a different body. Simplifying even further, fairies were souls. And after making a contract, fairies were souls that somehow had a link to the soul of a human. 

In that case, similar to how two humans can communicate with one another, couldn’t human souls communicate with fairy souls? If the human visualized something in their consciousness, wouldn’t the consciousness of the fairy be able to receive it and act accordingly?

I just assumed that Chiaros would only respond if I asked out loud what the time was, but what if I visualize that thought telepathically? 

Similarly, what if I just visualized firing a bow and arrow right now? Would it magically appear and fire? 

Six percent health. Akito closed his eyes. He didn’t really know if that would work, how to visualize it in a way that would get through to Chiaros. Though right now, he had no other choice. 

I guess I'll just do it like I write. Start with the first thought that comes to mind.

That was easy enough — he had a lot of practice. It wasn't long before he settled on a topic.

Rubio accepts someone as part of him by making a promise to them.
Maybe not on purpose, but I wrote him that way partially because of my own promise, didn’t I?

A week after Lazu had started off with “could this be fate”, Akito had topped up the second-hand embarrassment he received from that statement with some first-hand embarrassment. That day, Akito had asked Lazu about her circumstances, and she had replied that she was a human stuck on Complex Alpha.

How'd it go again? Something like this…?

Such a setting is perfect for the main character.
W-w-um, why? 
Have you read a story without conflict? That would be most boring indeed! 
W-well, I guess, b-but, you won't need it!
And why is that? Will you save me? Now that would be a nice dream. 

In hindsight, after all the teasing Lazu gave Akito for what he'd say next, Lazu probably meant it as a joke. But at that moment, Akito had leaned into the radio. 

I-I will! I'll save you! I promise! I’ll make your dream come true!

Now, with his health bar dropping to four percent, Akito cringed again upon recalling it. But then, he smiled. In the end, I didn't save you tomorrow. Or the day after. Really, it's almost been seven years since that day, and I still haven't fulfilled my promise.

The first glimmers of light began congregating at Akito's chest, near where his crest dimly shone. The light grew brighter and brighter, and then, in a flash, they moulded themselves into a sharp triangular tip. An arrowhead. The next instant, the shaft of the arrow materialized, then the fletchings. Bowstrings stretched out from the ring levitating above Akito's body, wrapping their tendrils along a slender nock. 

Akito opened his eyes. Exposed to his vulnerable underbelly, he pinpointed the vampire’s heart, which beat so furiously it could be spotted even through the strengthened body. Before the vampire could react, he aimed the bow directly at it.

There were two main ways to kill a vampire. The first was to inflict so many injuries on them that their regeneration couldn't help up. This was the most common way, though it was typically feasible only when fairies worked together. 

The second, meanwhile, required only one attack. It was to completely destroy the vampire’s heart before it had a chance to regenerate. This was far, far harder —the vampires’ toughened body meant that few attacks could even reach the heart, let alone completely destroy it in a near instant. 

Only, time stops for no one. <Yori no Mirai> was not of a spatial realm, and it could easily pierce through the vampire.

The vampire vaguely registered Akito’s bow and arrow in his peripheral vision. But his attention was on something else — he was transfixed by the dazzling glint in Akito’s eyes. The vampire thought to himself that Lapis had those same eyes. 

He hated those eyes. Or, well, he…

As Akito loosened his imaginary hold on the bowstrings, he continued his train of thought.

Seven years. Time flies, doesn’t it?

Time flies like a… 

Akito grinned, cutting himself off. No, no, no, that's terrible.

Only now did the vampire respond to the radiance building up beneath his underside. But it was too late. There was a violent explosion of light — a perfect light, one formed from the dawn of time. The arrow from the fourth dimension shot forth, urged forwards by words that came from deep within Akito’s soul. 

Yori no Mirai!

…

…

The vampire stumbled away, relaxing his grip on Akito, and Akito quickly fired his thrusters, putting a safe distance between them. His eyes met the vampire’s. For the first time in the fight, he noticed that unlike all the other vampires he had seen, this vampire had a miscoloured eye. 

One of his eyes was ruby red. The other was as pink as the cherry blossoms. 

For a moment, the vampire held his gaze. And then he staggered backwards, spread his wings, and took off into the sky. 

~

When Algernon and Minji found Akito amidst the swathe of destroyed cypresses, he didn't notice them approaching. 

Akito had transformed out of Chiaros. His eyes were closed, and he was resting peacefully against one of the few remaining trees in the area, uninjured despite the debris around him. Slouched besides him was Chiaros. He was in far worse shape to the point of near death: all four of his limbs dangled loosely from their joints, held together by thin strings of flesh alone, and his upper body was charred to the point that it couldn't be recognized as vaguely human. Despite that, he had an absentminded expression on his relatively uninjured face. 

Minji smiled upon seeing them. Chiaros would recover quickly, and the damage to Base 33 was minimal. It was a massive success. As it turned out, it would, by ask appearances, be the only success the human army would be able to claim. Code Name: Windbreak would not only force the human army to withdraw their offensive pressure, but it would also destroy eight of humanity’s fifty bases, all courtesy of the oni rampaging on land and making light work of the structures. And with the exception of the attack on Base 33, <Shadow> would suffer no fatalities.

But Minji would learn that later. For now, she turned and began to walk away, not wanting to disturb Akito. Algernon didn’t follow her. “Wait. What’s his name?” He asked as he pointed to the boy. Minji sighed; it had taken long enough. “Has anyone told you that you have a difficult personality?”

“Yep! All the women I’ve slept with.” Algernon replied with a laugh. Minji shook her head in annoyance before answering: “It’s Akito.”

“Akito, huh?” Algernon repeated the name, committing it into memory. “That person, Akito…He’ll change this war.”

Meanwhile, lying against the tree, Akito felt his consciousness drift into a quiet lull. As he let himself be taken by the peaceful flow, he tried to visualize a question within his soul, without words. What’s the time?

No answer at first. And then, besides him, a robotic voice answered out loud, ever so faintly. 

“It’s noon.”

~

In the entire archipelago, there was only one vampire that had one pink eye. Though, he usually kept it hidden behind an eyepatch, only revealing it in his vampiric transformation.

Mitsui flew low over the ocean, away from the coastline of the mainland. He had regenerated and healed all his major wounds. All except one: a hole, two meters in diameter, that started from his belly and perforated all the way to the other side. 

That wound, Mitsui knew, wouldn’t heal. Because somewhere within that hole was the location where Chiaros’ arrow had perfectly pierced his heart, decimating it in an instant and leaving no trace behind. 

Even without a heart, a vampire could continue functioning for a few more minutes — a testament to their incredibly resilient body. Though right now, Mitsui thought that was a bit unfortunate. As he glided absentmindedly to no destination in particular, his thoughts drew him towards Akito’s eyes, just before he shot his arrow. And that, in turn, drew him to thoughts of the main character. 

The night before, Lapis had asked to meet him alone. She had sat in silence for a long time, so long that Mitsui began to wonder why she had even called him in the first place. And then, as she fiddled with her pendant, she had asked him if there was anything he wanted to say to her. A question, where they both knew the answer.

Mitsui did have things he wanted to say. He told her how he hated her, Erin, and Evie for stealing the spotlight, how he felt invisible next to them. They were his ugliest thoughts, because deep down, he knew it was something akin to jealousy, jealousy and resignation. Even still, Lapis listened without saying a word. Until finally, after he was finished and she had sat in silence for a bit longer, they began talking back and forth. 

Now, Mitsui recalled the words Lapis had said near the end of the conversation, after he had denounced her for proclaiming that he was equally as dazzling.

“Everyone is the main character. I’ll prove that to you, Mitsui. I promise.”

And therein lay the contradiction in Mitsui’s thoughts. 

He believed Lapis, the supreme commander of the army, and his friend, was the main character. Because of that, he hated her for being one of the lead actors on the stage of life, constantly rendering him unseen. 

But it was because of that same belief, that he knew she would keep her promise. That someday, she would show him he was a main character, not an invisible background in the stories of others. He hated her, yet believed she would show him that his hate was misplaced.

Why is it that the ones I hated were the ones that truly saw me?
Oh, right…
They saw me.

Mitsui felt the noon sunlight beat down on his broad back, felt the sea breeze caress his wings and guide him somewhere far away. He stopped thinking about Lapis and himself. There was no point. 

As his vision cut in and out of darkness, Mitsui murmured to himself. “It’s bright…”

Those were his last words. Shortly after, he lost consciousness, never to wake again. His lifeless body crashed into the ocean like a pebble thrown into a pond. 

…

…

…

~

That night, Akito sat excitedly in front of the radio, telling Lazu all about his heroics that noon. As usual, Lazu laughed, asked questions, and interjected with her teasing from time to time. Only Tanner thought to himself that Lazu seemed a bit off today. 

After she turned the radio off, Lapis walked over to her desk. She pulled out a map of the archipelago from one of the lower drawers and made a mark on the coast of the mainland. It had been easy to correlate Akito’s story with the information she had received as commander of the army, and now, she muttered: “Now I know where Base 33 is…”

Lapis stared at the map a moment longer before putting it away and exiting her room. She walked down the corridors of the living quarters and knocked on a room.

“Blue?” 

For a few seconds, no one answered. Just as Lapis turned to leave, the door unlocked and opened. Blue met Lapis’ gaze, her hair slightly unkempt from lying on the bed. “Yeah?”

“Nothing. Just thought I’d check in on you.”

Shortly after they landed back on Complex Alpha, one of the Nightjar pilots had shared the news with Lapis. That Code Name: Windbreak was a major success, with the exception of the attack on one of the bases on the western side of the battlefield. That they had reports on all three assailants — one vampire, one elf, and one oni — being killed. The Nightjar pilot had shared the names of the vampire and elf, names which Lapis had already known from the moment he said which base it was. After all, she was responsible for the attack. 

Blue was with Lapis at the time. She heard the news as well. 

Now, Blue quietly walked back inside, leaving the door open for Lapis to follow. Lapis closed the door behind them, and they sat down next to each other on the bed. For a long time, neither spoke. And then, Blue began tentatively. “Lapis,” she hesitated, before choosing to go forward, “That night, on Erin’s birthday, Mitsui said he hated all of you.”

Lapis nodded. “I know. I lied about not eavesdropping. I was listening.”

“Oh.” Blue looked up at Lapis in surprise, before dropping her gaze. She paused, figuring out how to best word her question. “Why…why did he hate you?” 

“He thought we shone too bright. Everyone focused on us and treated him like he was invisible.”

Blue stared at her, trying to process her words. “But I didn’t!” Lapis let out a wry smile. “He was an idiot who couldn’t realize that he was a main character in our stories. That’s what I told him last night.”

“You talked to him?” Lapis nodded again, and Blue pondered her words once more. Finally, she asked: “Were you two able to work things out?”

“No.” Lapis admitted. “But I think we started to.”

The two let the silence stretch out between them once more. They were silent a lot tonight. Blue found the silence a bit comforting. 

At some point, Lapis turned to Blue, studying the fairy’s face. “Blue, you okay?” Blue gave a small nod and Lapis smiled briefly.

It was an odd smile. Blue spent minutes trying to figure out what was odd about it. But just before she realized what to say, Lapis leapt from the bed and landed gracefully on the floor with a thud. “Did you know, Blue?” Her voice was strong, loud enough to fill up the whole room. 

“The main character needs tragedy and suffering! That makes the ending that much sweeter!” She grinned, one which shone brightly, like the artificial ceiling light above them. “Development like this is perfect. This too, is part of the script!”

“...”

Blue couldn’t say anything as Lapis ruffled her hair. “So don’t go all depressed pillbug on me, Blue. We have a war to win!

~

After Lapis left Blue’s bedroom, Blue peeked out cautiously, confirming that she truly wasn’t eavesdropping. Then, she locked the door, pulling on it twice to make sure it was shut. 

Blue sat on her bed and studied her wings. Lapis had described them as tacky decorations, and it was true she couldn’t fly with them. But they served another function purpose. The crystals that comprised the wings were pouches that only Blue could open and close. Now, she opened the crystal closest to her fairy crest, taking out its contents and placing it on the bed.

It was a handheld radio. The model used by the human army. 

Blue pressed a few buttons, and she connected to the other end. Someone immediately greeted her. “Hey, Alice,” the voice began, before adding with a taste of disdain, “Or, well, Bluebird.”

“Sorry I’m late, Algernon.” Blue replied. She was used to the general of the human army being particular about names, and he had already expressed plenty of contempt with the name she had received as a fairy.

Ever since Blue had moved from her jail cell to a room with lockable doors and privacy, she had met with Algernon Prischett routinely. Of course she had.

Bluebird of Flight was a spy for the human army. 

Today, Algernon was in an excellent mood. 

“Blue, thanks for telling us about Windbreak! Just about everything went according to plan! We minimized casualties and allowed <Shadow> to destroy a few of our bases.” He laughed. “Well, one thing didn't go according to plan. Some boy named Akito showed us up. He'll be an important one. I can tell.”

Blue must have been distracted by other things. Because she didn’t respond even as Algernon called her name. It took the fourth try for Algernon to receive an apologetic “Sorry. What?”

“Did something happen? Are you okay?”

The spy stayed silent for a moment, the question weighing down in her mind. It was the same question Lapis had asked her. The one she hadn’t asked back. And then, she smiled and reassured Algernon. “I’m fine.”

“It’s just…I didn’t think people would actually die.”













Part 2: FIN

12:30 ALICE | BLUEBIRD
Rip Mitsui
